We had $25,000 left in the bank. We were going to have to shut down the company. It was my identity as a founder. I didn't know where to go from here. I didn't know what the next year would look like. And 3 days later, that video went viral. I remember reloading our subscriptions and everyone on that first page [music] was the same day. And I was like, am I reading that right? I'm like, this is a bug. And it didn't stop. We went from making 3500 in October. [music] In November, we made over $21,000. >> And then 3 months later, >> 4 months later, we hit 2 million. [music] >> So, what happened next? Guys, I am super excited to announce, but we have this series called What's Your Dream? Where we help people make their dreams come true. Now, we don't do these very often because, well, they're very timeconuming, but we have just completed one of them and we are looking for our next dream to support. Now, if this sounds like you and you have a big dream that you need support in, let us know down below in the comments and you may just hear from one of us. All right, guys, let's get back to the video. >> [music] >> as a whole, what do you wish that people knew in business? >> This is a good question because there's so many things that I could tell you, but for us, one of the biggest things was making a very clear mission about who we're for. So, instead, when you're thinking about your business, a lot of times you'll use another business to compare it to. So, for us, we really model ourselves after Notion. And Notion's audience is everyone. Like, everyone could use Notion. And we were like, well, we'll be for everyone on social media. So, anyone that wants to use social media as a business, whether you work on a podcast, um, as a content creator, influencer, talent manager, social media manager, in-house team, no matter who it is, you can use REA. And that was such a mistake because when you're first starting out, you don't really have an identity. So, because we were so broad, no one knew who we were for, we built a Frankenstein of a product, I always say, because we added these features for all of these different types of people. And then sometimes influencers would use us and be like, "Wait, what is this feature over here?" And we'll be like, "Oh, don't worry about that. That's not for you." And it's like, "Oh, then why is it in the app?" And it's like, "Oh, well, it's for this other subset of audience." And vice versa, it would just confuse people. And it caused turn. No one knew who we were for. So they would just, you know, delete the app, not use us, not pay for us, not recommend us cuz they were even confused about who we were for. And then when we would launch Rella 2.0, you know, we decided we're going to be for social media managers and that's it. And that made the biggest difference. And like we had investors tell us this. We had advice that was like start small, start small, start small. But that like limiting belief or whatever it is of being like well if I start small then I'm missing out on this big opportunity of like everyone else that could possibly use us and that really hurt us. So I would say to anyone start as small as possible and then once you do a good job with that really small group of people then expand from there. But how do you identify which is the right group of people to start with? >> So when we first started, we were specifically for influencers and that was like our very very very first person. Then we realized, oh, influencers aren't paying us. They're not sticking around. Let's be for influencers and blank. And then same thing, like they weren't really sticking around. They didn't see us. Okay, so we're going to add another group of people. So I think you can test who you're for, but only one at a time. Like don't test everyone at once or else it's going to become this big mess. Um, but the question we asked ourselves was, okay, influencers weren't paying for us. So, that was clear. Like, they weren't paying for us. They weren't using us. Anytime we showed it to an influencer, they would say, "Oh my god, love it. This is exactly what I need." And then we'd be like, "Okay, great. Here it is. Crickets." No one would use us. So, I think what we realized was there is a difference between saying that someone needs something and like saying that they have the problem, but then once you start testing it and putting it in the market and asking for a credit card and they don't start doing it, then you know that the willingness to pay isn't there. And if you're not making money as a business, you have a hobby. Like because of that, we were like, okay, well, influencers aren't paying us. So that's when we started asking ourselves, who has the biggest problem and who is willing to pay for this problem? And then those two together led us to social media managers and teams. I think most cases people try to change behavior thinking that it's a lot easier to do that versus just seeing where behavior is already, paying for it, and then going there because it'll be a lot. It's like almost like going with a a current, right? >> Yeah. And I completely agree because for so long I was like this is so much better than your spreadsheet that you're using. Like why are you not using this? And it was too hard to change the behavior of people. And realistically, are you going to change the behavior of the masses in the short amount of time that you have for your runway or your funding unless your product is like 10x better then it's probably not going to happen. Like the reason why the iPhone or Chat JBT really changed consumer behavior is because it was so good. So for a lot of first-time founders, I would recommend instead of saying like our product needs to be 10x better than my competitors, your customer's pain points need to be 10x bigger than you think it should be. That's how you can kind of see like is this a big enough pain point for my audience is that even though this product kind of glitches sometimes and isn't the best yet, people are still using this over the existing tools that are out there because we're solving this problem. We have whites space in the market. And that's what we found at RELA was that the pain point of social media managers and teams was so deep that even though our product wasn't the absolute best in terms of you know we had bugs and sometimes it glitched and whatever it was still better than what they were currently doing. >> How do you know you have a good business idea when you haven't tested the painoint yet? >> I think there's a lot you can do before you launch or before you spend time on it. So building up weight lists, talking to customers, and actually seeing how they interact in the day-to-day, having interviews with them, asking them, watching their vlogs, watching their content, and you having to make that decision of like, is this delusional in me, thinking they'll actually use this, like because I love it and I would use it, or is this something they would actually like, I could actually see fitting in here? >> What if they steal your idea if you post it online? >> I don't think stealing an idea is something you need to worry about. That might be a hot take, but I always think that if you are the person with the idea, if you're the one that's already been obsessed with this and done research and started making moves, it's going to be hard for someone to just come in and be like, "Oh, yeah, that's actually a really good idea. Let me drop everything I'm doing right now and start doing this." Like, the odds of that happening, one is rare, and two, if someone does steal your idea, they're never going to understand the intricacies that you understand about the problem or the pain point. So, it might look different. it's they're going to execute it differently. And I think if someone is just a copycat and like steals ideas, they probably don't have it in them to be a long-lasting founder because they're not authentic. They're not going to know what to do next. They're not going to have the next steps. And if they are just waiting on you to constantly copy, you're always going to be one step ahead of them. So you you hit 1 million in revenue in 11 months from now from starting from for the 2.0. Correct. >> Right. So we scratched the first product completely. We took it off the app store. We started from zero. We had zero users when we relaunched. From there we hit a million in ARR. So annual recurring revenue. >> And then it 3 months later, >> four months later we hit 2 million. >> That's impressive. What are you doing right now like in this moment that you guys are doubling down on that you think is so effective? And I think that was very intentional. That was not an accident that just happened. And then now I think the number one thing we do is we listen to our users and we move really quickly. So if our users face an issue, we are fixing that right away. Like we move fast. With that comes sometimes bugs or you know things that we maybe move too quickly and then our users email us and they're like this isn't working anymore. And we're like oh my god we need to fix this. So then we'll go back and you know fix everything. But I would rather that than have a bunch of meetings deciding what's next on our road map and what can we do and then the product is sitting sale for 3 months and people are turning and people are emailing us and then it's going to customer support overseas. Like we don't do that. I still do our customer support inbox me and Natasha and Alexis. And it has helped me so much as a leader and as the CEO and as a founder like hearing what our customers don't like has been the biggest thing because when we do talk to customers the number one thing they say is oh my god I love how you listen to us and that's what I want to be known for because if we are building a tool for social media teams and then we're not listening to social media teams then what are we doing? That is like super super important to us is listening to our customers. >> A lot of what you're saying is like I'm going to just double down on what's working. Right. >> Exactly. And we don't want to let that go cuz the second you do things will start to slip. Like we put smiley faces and emojis and like we're just like enthusiastic, you know, emailers and texters. Like we can't be that agency that just like starts letting them go cuz we have them. >> Exactly. We're like loyal RA customers. Our customers are the only reason why we're here and we owe everything to them. So, that's how we really like try to reframe it. >> Yeah. Yeah, I mean I think that a lot of people I know for us we really were in a rush to be act like a big business way before we were and I think that that actually harmed us in a lot of ways and so what do you think are the benefits to you know staying agile versus having a you know sprints for example specifically within the app? >> Yeah. So, I totally agree that too many people look to these big businesses and think, "Oh, okay, they're doing it, so we must do it." And it's so different because these big businesses have had years of experience under their belt. They have systems. They know it works for them. They have a large team. So, large teams need to work very differently than small teams. And I actually think there's such a privilege of being a small team. Like, I fear for the day that we have to be bigger. Like, I love working in a small team. And so, we work more in a agile way. I would definitely advise that small teams don't need to be like big teams. I love the Paul Graham, I believe it's Paul Graham, the quote of do things that don't scale, which means we do our customer support. We are very personable. The people that use us know who the team is because I'm on the social media. I'm posting about us. That is we live by that. You know, speaking of social, I am really intrigued because I think that with your background, you really understand it obviously on a whole different level than most founders do. And I think that that was a huge obviously that was a very big part of the success of Rea. Um, what what do you think is the future of social media? I think we are still in the beginning stages of social media, believe it or not. I know it's like everywhere and in your face, but I do still think it's the beginning and we're going to start seeing everything on social. Like traditional media, I think, will kind of go away and it's going to be replaced by things like YouTube, for example. Why would I watch a late night talk show when I can watch this? >> Yeah. >> On my own time with someone I'm actually interested in. It's curated for me. it came up on my algorithm. Like, why why not? And with businesses, they need to start relying more on people resonating with them and relating to them and wanting to consume the the media that they're creating. Every single business needs to have a creator. Whether that's the founder, whether it's like an in-house creator, attention is the currency now. Like, you have to just capture people's attention. And I also think that's how you build community by capturing their attention, having someone be like, "Wait, I relate to that or I like that or I want to see more of that." And that's how you start building it and and providing value also. And you see that now with you go into Sephora and 10 years ago it was Makeup Forever. It was all these other brands. Now you go in and it's Makeup by Mario. It's celebrity and creator le brands now. And that's because of social media. >> So let's say somebody's listening, right? And they're like, "Okay, fine. Talk about what you're building and what problem you're solving and show people how you're doing it. It does not have to be good quality. It just has to be letting people in behind the curtain because that's never been done before. Like you're maybe heads down founder, hands- on keyboard, hire someone that can do it. >> What is the metric that they are tracking? >> You need to make sure that you're establishing connections with your ideal customer. Like, it's one of those things where you have to be consistent with it. But, I think it's about community and connection. And sometimes it's not a cutand clear metric. Like, the ROI isn't there immediately or the metric isn't necessarily views or anything like that. I think it's more of just like, do you feel like you're building a community? Like, are people tuning in? Are they invested in this? Are they wanting to see more? And too many people stop before they see the results. It's kind of like that. I would try random things until something does work. And I don't think it has to go viral. I think it just needs to like reach your ideal audience and have like some more engagement. Like the video for Rea, we had a video go viral. We say it got like 60k views in in the first day, which is good. Like that's objectively good, but like when you think viral, you think a million or 2 million or 3 million. For us, it was 60K and that 7x our revenue. So eventually ended up getting more views, but we put paid ads behind it, but organic content and building an organic community is something that I would start with because I know some people might say like, "Oh, we're just going to run a bunch of ads and see what sticks." And that's fair. Like you can do that and you might get a customer, but I do think nowadays, like for us, we don't run ads separate from our organic content. Like, you know, some people make ads specifically for ads and then they post different stuff for their organic. Everything that we run an ad on has already been posted organically, and we typically only run ads on things that did well organically. So, before we're wasting money spending it on ads that aren't going to perform well, we've tested it with our audience. The algorithms will tell you what your audience wants to see. >> Why do you think the video that went viral uh went viral? >> Because it didn't feel like salesy or like an ad and it was like a relatable problem. So Steve Jobs, you know, again I mentioned that we recently did a video on him and there's this speech where he talks about connecting the dots. um he gave it at Stanford and he talks about how when he looks back at his life all of these points in the moment they didn't make sense but when he looks back at it if you were to take out one of those things he wouldn't be where he was today. So that being said what parts of your journey were pivotal in Rea's creation? Ever since I was a kid I've always been very entrepreneurial before knowing what being an entrepreneur was. Like I wanted to be a writer when I was little. So I first started wanting to be an author. That was like my first thing that I wanted. >> Your first dream. >> Yes. My first dream. And I think it was because one, I loved reading because I loved stories. Like storytelling was my favorite thing. Then I wanted to be an actress. So that was my next dream. I went to like acting classes. I got an agent. I like went to LA for auditions. Like that was my dream when I was a little kid. And I wanted to be on Disney Channel. Like that was my goal. And then when I was in high school, YouTube came around and I saw girls that were like doing their makeup on YouTube. I was like, "This is so cool." Like, I think I had I got three subscribers the first day and I was like, "Oh my god, I can't believe three people followed me. Like, that's so crazy." And then I did engineering in college because my parents really wanted me to do that and I was like good at math and liked it. So, I'm glad I did it because I think it did make me like more of a problem solver. After college, I ended up working for a consulting company. And then I ended up quitting that consulting job because I was making double my salary. I was making $120,000 a year being an influencer, became a full-time influencer, started an agency, and that all led me to REA. So, like, if all of those things didn't happen, I feel like now I look back at my life and I'm like, that makes sense. Like, I'm doing REA is the culmination of all of those things >> for sure. Like, it is like all of them make sense together and they make what you're doing >> and they make no sense individually. >> No, they don't. Like if you're just like this and this this is this person's resume, it's like that's really weird. But now that I'm doing what I'm doing, it it does make sense. >> Walk me through how you go from idea of reel to then finding your co-founders. >> Yeah. So I'm not technical like I don't know how to code. So I had this idea and in the beginning stage of any business idea that I have, I do research on it and I'm like how do I do this? So I was like looking up like how much does it cost? What are the next steps? And I was like, "Okay, so I definitely need developers to do this. This isn't like a project that I can just like assign to someone." So I asked one of my really good friends. I was like, "Do you know anyone at NC State that knows how to code that I could talk to that cuz I didn't know anyone." And he was like, "Yeah, actually this one guy that I know, one of my friends, he started a software consultancy company if you want to talk to him." And he was the first one that like didn't give me cut and dry answers of like, "Oh yeah, it's going to cost this much and it's going to take me this amount of time." He was like, "Uh, I don't know how long this is going to take. I don't know how much it's going to cost, but like we can do this in increments and see because we need to like scope out the like scope of work. Like he was the first one that actually laid it out as like more than just a project and of this like long-term thing. And so we started working together and then we really loved working together. So we ended up becoming co-founders and that's how we formed REA. But it was a lot of interviews of like a lot of other people and then this was one where I just like trusted my gut. Like again, I do think it's a lot of intuition. Like being a founder is making a million decisions every single day. You are not going to have all of the data. You're not going to have all of the insight. Like you just need to trust your gut sometimes and learn and make mistakes along the way. >> Once you guys decided to work together, did you both go full-time or were they still working their agency? Were you still doing your agency at the time? >> So, they did roll that off, but then they had to get other jobs while we were fundraising. And like I was still doing social media full-time as well. We didn't have any money. Like realistically, I don't know how some people like go all in with their company if they're not making money yet. I'm like, "And do you have like a trust fund or like what are you using doing to survive?" Because yeah, they they were working other jobs while they were working other like dev jobs while we were trying to fundra. And then once we fundraised, they got paid first and I still was making money from social media. So I was like, I want to extend runway for this company, so I'm going to hold off until I really need to. And I got paid like a little bit in the beginning and then when we weren't growing that much I was like I'm gonna wait like I don't want to get paid right now. So for like over two years I wasn't getting paid and I was just working on social media as my income. Once we became profitable this year I started getting paid again in May. So >> that's the reality of May of 2025 is when I first started getting paid. >> I don't know if you experienced this but on when I got announced on Forbes my mom had to call my family and be like by the way that's her company making that money. like she doesn't have that in her bank account for like no one to think that I was just like swimming in all this money. The baby eats first. The business goes first, your employees, your team. Like it really is the priority in order for it to actually build and be scalable. And I think that that a lot of people don't realize like sure you have this asset that is worth something. So that means something, but at the end of the day, like you are probably the least paid employee out of everyone. you are probably working more hours than anyone else with no OT and it's just the the reality of building something. So in reality you just keep pouring it in. Sure eventually take some out but >> you know yeah none of us are making really high salaries like my co-founders could get other tech jobs for much more than they are making at REA but it's the reality of us you know wanting to build something together something that outlasts you. So then here's the predicament for someone else. my current income is the thing that you know funds this dream but it's also distracting me from making real progress in this dream. What should I do? >> You just have to find a balance of it. Everyone's schedule is different but if you can work on the weekends, if you can wake up earlier, if you can go to bed later, it just depends on your situation. Like I can't tell you, oh, quit your job and go full-time because like I wouldn't recommend that. I would not recommend quitting your job and having no money and trying something that might not work. that 90% of businesses don't work, you know, like I wouldn't recommend putting all your eggs in one basket. I think there's nothing wrong in having side hustles. So, yeah, I would try to find a way to make it work. And you might have to sacrifice or you will have to sacrifice some areas in your life, whether that's your social life, whether that's your weekends, then you sacrifice it. But I don't think that you should just like quit and have no income cuz then you're running on burnout also and and you're so stressed and that's not the best way to run a business either. >> No, totally. I completely agree. Um, okay. So, you go on after this. You guys, while you were seeking investment, were you building the app or did you wait to have an MVP before getting investors? >> No. So, we built the app before we took on investors. So, we built the app, we had an MVP, we had traction. We didn't have paying customers yet. We were a free app, but we did have the app in the app store um while we raised money. >> And you got the traction from social media. >> Yes. Mhm. So everything was organic social media, but to be honest, we had traction, but because we weren't a paid app, we were looking at vanity metrics. Like we were looking at downloads and signups and like that doesn't matter truly. Like I don't even know how many signups we get on right now. All I know is how many people converted from the free trial to paid. I think that if someone's downloading your app and you have a million downloads, okay, but how many people paid for it? We were on the app store the first day on like the app store charts, but like that didn't really mean anything in terms of the success of the business. >> Do you remember more or less how many downloads until you were able to go to investors or could you have gone sooner even if there were no downloads? >> I think we had 3,000 customers that had signed up and created an account. We turned on paid subscriptions and got like three people. >> The the investors saw that through that you think at the time >> we didn't turn on paid before we raised. So that was something we actually were told which I don't know we raised in 2022 so I don't know if this is the advice today but we were like don't turn on paid yet because then investors are going to have a lot more questions. They're going to have questions about why was this conversion so low? What about the when you're a free app investors can dream? >> You feel like that was a mistake. Would you do it differently now? >> I don't regret anything that we did but if I were to start a business today I wouldn't do that. I would try not to raise money and if I did, I would still try to make revenue right away because I just think that like it's hard to have a sustainable business if you're not take getting any money right away. And like money is what is going to tell you if you need to pivot or not. That's going to be the growth metric. Downloads does not matter. Signups does not matter. Who is willing to pay? Who has a big enough problem that they are willing to pay for this? >> That being said, how did you begin looking for investors? So, I didn't know anything about raising capital, like literally nothing. One of the best podcasts I listened to was the startup podcast. Um, it's filmed like probably 10 years ago at this point, but it was so helpful. So, that really put me in the understanding of like what's an investor meeting like or anything like that. I started asking other founders like if they would just jump on calls with me and be like, "Hey, can you explain to me like what was your fundraising process like?" And like how was that? And then I would always ask the founders I was meeting if they could introduce me to their investors or any investors that they knew. So then that's how I got a lot of intros. But I would cold email those people like cold email cold DM. I would look on Crunchb. I got a Crunchb subscription. I would look at people that were similar to us and I would see who invested in them. I would look at active investors investing in preede stage funds in creator economy. Like I did so many different searches for that and then I compiled that list and did a ton of cold outreach. How many investors more or less would you say that you reached out to? >> Oh my gosh. Well, we spoke to like 200. So, I probably reached out to like a thousand. Mhm. And we got five yeses out of 200. >> So, this is officially the part of um the show where we want to do show and tell and you were so gracious enough to share your pitch deck that got a million dollars in fundraising, right? It really does help us create better videos, bring on great guests, and most importantly, keeping the lights on. Okay, so this was Re 1.0 when we were a tool for influencers. So this is no longer our product. We raised on the first version of the product and then we pivoted. We had the title slide with what are we doing? So we make it easier for content creators to run their business. We started by saying that everyone wants to be an influencer because we wanted investors to know that even if they don't understand the creator economy, this is a big deal and like investors need to be paying attention or they're going to fall behind. >> This is so cool. >> Yeah. And then again to educate investors and to show them how big of a pain point this is, I show the workflow of an influencer. So they're on multiple platforms. Millions of influencers, so millions of influencers, big market are using fragmented and inefficient tools to run their business. I also tried to compare it to other industries because again, a lot of this was educating investors on the influencer space because they just didn't know. And all of these businesses that I show have billiondoll plus valuations because investors want to know that this could become a billion-dollar company. And then I have the solution which was REA 1.0. So Rea integrates with an influencer's entire workflow. So we connect and organize all the platforms. We track earnings, invoices, and estimate future revenue. And you can view and share your comprehensive analytics. It's so crazy seeing like what Really used to look like. And then the why now. So why is now a good time? Because investors want to know like why should they invest now? Like what's the market looking like? The amount of influencers is increasing every year. Marketers are investing more money into influencer marketing and content creators are the fastest growing small businesses. So I wanted investors to know like yes this is a tool for individual influencers but like they are businesses. So this is a tool for small businesses. And then I showed our traction. We had over 10,000 downloads 5,000 community across platforms. So that was like our followers. And then we didn't spend any money in marketing. So, this was to show like we got all of this traction without even spending money on marketing. Imagine what we could do if we did spend money on marketing. >> Was this the slide that they cared about most or they responded to the most, you would say? >> Yes. And it's definitely the one that they had the most questions about, too, cuz they were like, "Okay, you're not spending money on marketing. How are you getting customers?" And they also wanted to know like, "Okay, well, is it just you? Is it just your following?" Because like that's going to cap out at some point. So, I had to explain like, "No, we've built this community that's like that lives outside of me, too." Then I said, "Why us?" So investors want to make sure that there's founder market fit. So like why are you the team to build it? Because like anyone can build this. So like what makes you different? And investors are investing in the team. They know that the product will probably change. Like they know that it's going to evolve. They want to invest in the team itself. So this was where the why us came into play. For why us, I talked about myself about how I've been an influencer since 2011. Uh I've made it my full-time business. I quit my job when I made 2x my salary so that they know that there's money in this in the top 6% of influencers on YouTube, spoken with hundreds of influencers about their pain points and experiences and grew frustrated the lack of tools made for content creators. So I explained like I am the right person to build this. And then this was my favorite slide. It was what our users were saying. As you can tell we were very customer oriented from the very beginning. I mentioned we were built for influencers by influencers. So again how community is so important to us and I mentioned we host unpaid and voluntary focus groups because again that's what I mentioned like so many of these weed companies it's like I'll give you 20 bucks if you talk to us and it's like I don't want to have to pay for my users to talk to us. I hope my users just like want to talk to us. And then I mentioned our competitive landscape. So kind of like where we are set apart. So we're for small businesses and influencers. And then the ask, we were asking for one and a half million at this point, but we ended up raising 1 million. That would give us 24 months of runway. So my co-founders, we now have added Natasha as a co-founder, but at this point it was the three of us. And yeah, that was our pitch deck. >> That was awesome. That was so great. Yeah. So, if you had to quantify it, what makes a great pitch? >> The story. I think too many people look up Canva template pitch deck. They just like templatize it and they start putting their own stuff in it and then they try to make the story out of the deck and then it just doesn't sound good. You have to tell the story first because most of the times when you're pitching in front of people, you're talking, they're paying attention to you. The slides are just extra. It doesn't have to be in order of what the standard pitch decks look like. Say it out loud and then add the slides later. And investors hear pitches all day every day. That is their job. So how is yours going to stand out? It has to give them an emotional reaction. It cannot just be like, "Oh, good numbers." Like it has to be a story that they are excited about but like feel something towards. So you have to make that connection with them. That's why our story required a lot of education because if we didn't have that then like a lot of investors it would have fallen flat and I'm like well I don't feel anything towards this cuz I don't know. But if you're like oh my gosh wait I I have a kid and my kid watches Mr. Beast all the time and he wants to be a YouTuber. Oh okay. Like is this tool going to help my kid make money in the future? Like you have to find a way to relate to them in that sense. >> How do you get through 200 pitches and not quit? >> Yeah. I had this motto that I stuck with that I reminded my team about. It's not if, it's when. So, it's not if we raise, we're going to raise. It's just when we end up raising. And that has been my motto for so many things in life. It's not if, it's when. But it was hard because like with my co-founders, they're developers. So, they're writing code, pushing an update, you can see their work. For me, yeah, I had all these meetings, but I had zero dollars. I had no yeses. So, it was like, yeah, I had these meetings with investors. And it's like, well, what came from it? nothing. >> But the progress was there. It was invisible, but it was there. >> Yeah, exactly. And that was just really hard. And I think that's honestly even my role today though. Like a lot of the things that I'm doing, I'm not again I'm not hands to keyboard coding. Like the only output you see is like my social media content, but everything else is making deeper connections, doing things that don't scale, talking to our customers, listening to them, like it's not that direct output. And I think that can sometimes be hard because I'm like, am I doing a good job? Am I doing enough? Am I what am I working on? You know, and I think that's a challenge of being a CEO or a founder is if you don't have that like direct output of this is what it pushed out. It's just all like, well, I hope that what I did works. So, walk me through the day. I want you to like describe it to me, the feeling. How do you find out that you get your investment? What are some mistakes that you made other than, you know, like audience and all of that, but like are there any other mistakes that you made along the way during that that season? >> We made so many mistakes. Um, we hired someone that wasn't the right hire at one point and we had to let them go a month later after hiring them. That was a huge mistake. Listening to people when they're like, "This is such a good idea. If only you had this one more feature, then I would use you." And we're like, like it was just like we constantly were like chasing people to like beg them to use the product. All of those things like outweigh all the tiny bad decisions that we made. >> And now we come to that part of your story where you approaching the 24 months. At that point, have you guys pivoted yet to rea 2.0? >> No. So we were not growing. We had money until January and this was in May. So we had like six, seven months left of runway. Let's have a Hail Mary of trying to get something to work and then if that doesn't work, we can truly say that we did everything we could and we gave it a good run. >> So, at this point, you had no idea what that new thing was. >> No, we just knew that we needed to start something new. We came up with it pretty quickly. We sat down really the conversation was like, okay, who is going to be our new like target customer? And we discovered that by like who's willing to pay? Social media managers. Social media managers have the biggest problem. They want a tool that they can plan their content. they can send content to their clients without their clients having any friction and then they want to be able to distribute it amongst all the social media platforms and that was like very simple easy to understand we modeled the UX after like project management tools again not reinventing the wheel honestly in the beginning you should kind of look like other tools because then they're familiar with it there's not a learning curve and then we took 2 months and we built that so we built RA 1.0 in like a year and then we built RA 2.0 know in like 2 months. We kept saying like why didn't we do this sooner only we had more time. We had until January like until we were like out of money. >> So what happened next? >> We launched in August. It got a good amount of awareness like not a ton, but we got I think our first month we maybe made like $3,000 or less. So then we relaunched in August and did better than you know what we saw for the previous app for like when just relaunching. And then in September we hit like I don't know maybe again around 3,000 or something. I don't fully remember all the numbers. And then October is when we hit 3500. We had nothing left. And for a startup, that's really not a lot. We were paying three people like Natasha, Nick, and Connor. We're getting paid with that. So we had two months. So we decided that things aren't working and we're going to have to shut down. So, this was after the relaunch already. We told our investors. And then my co-founders were applying for new jobs. So tell me about where you like how you were feeling in that time. >> Um it was probably one of the hardest times that we ever had to go through because it was like a baby, you know? It was like a living breathing thing. Something we worked so hard on. It was my identity as a founder. Like it was like I am a business owner. Like that's what people know me as now. Like I was embarrassed cuz I built in public and to then show like oh it didn't work out. What am I going to do when I don't have this? Like it was also like a a crutch and then I didn't know where to go from here. I didn't know what the next year would look like. It was like a road with no destination for the first time in my life. And it truly was one of the darkest weeks of my life when we decided that we were going to have to shut down the company. And then I kind of got to acceptance of being like we're all going to be fine and like businesses fail and I did everything I could. Like I I just had to think like logically about it and not emotional about it. So I kind of like numbed it a little bit. >> Was it to cope? >> I think so. It was very much like I'm not going to think about the future. Like I didn't even know what I was going to do in 2025 because this was again end of 2024. I had no idea what I was going to do. I was just like I'm not going to think about it. I'm just going to live like dayto-day. So, it was a weird time when we were still showing up to work. We were still doing things knowing that the end was near because we're like, "What's the point?" Like, if a customer had a bug, we were like, "What's the point?" Like, like, "We're literally out of business in 2 months. What are we doing?" And I was like, "No, have to have that do that. We're not going to be out of business." So, this conversation happened on a Thursday in October. Friday, I talked to Natasha and our investors. Monday, that video went viral. So, 3 days later, >> did you think it was going to go viral when you were filming it? No, I had no idea because at this point we had given up. Like we were pretty down in the dumps. We were Yeah, it was more of like, well, we're just posting it. So, no, I did not think it. But I am very persistent. I don't like to give up. So, even though we pretty much threw our hat in the ring and we were like, I think this company is shutting down in January, we still continued. And that video when we thought we were shutting down is what ended up skyrocketing us. And again, it didn't got 60K views in the first day. Like it wasn't crazy. It wasn't anything like insane. I remember going through our subscriptions and like usually we would have like one person that signed up for like a free trial a day. Some days we wouldn't have anyone. Some days we'd have three people. Like if we had five people sign up for a free trial, I was like, "Wo, that's a good day for us." And I was like, am I reading that right? I'm like, this is a bug. Like this is like not right because we always get just like one person and I would refresh and then like new people and I was like, what? And I texted the team and I'm like, why did 30 new people just sign up for a free trial? Like the demos were booked out for that week. I was like, whoa. And then we got emails like some people were emailing us questions and I was like, something is happening. Like this is crazy. And it didn't stop that month. Every single day we were getting 20 new people, 30 new people. Like it was like every day we were growing. And I was like, "Well, we have to wait two weeks to see if they convert because that's we have a two week free trial." So, we're like, "Let's see if they can." >> Oh my god, that's so stressful. >> I had no idea. Like, obviously, I could see if they were like canceling right away, but it didn't look like it. And I was like, "This is kind of crazy." So, then in November, we went from making $3500 in October. In November, we made over $21,000 that month from the free trials. And then in December, I think we made like 28,000. And then in January, we hit like 30 something,000. February we were profitable. Like it happened fast and we were like whoa. Like we have more time than January, so like let's keep doing this. And again, I was like, is this just like a fluke? Like is this just kind of like a flash in the pan? Like how long can we sustain this? So we were still very conservative with our burn. Like I was not paying myself until May. Even though we were hit profitability in February, I was like, I'm we need to keep this up. Thank you. Thank you. All right, guys. We are diving into the anatomy of Natalie's dream. We are going to be going through all of the principles that popped up in her journey. And because there are a lot of principles that came up in Natalie's journey, specifically principles that we've talked about in previous episodes, we are going to list them all right here. What we're going to do is highlight the ones that we feel are the most important to highlight for you. And with that being said, let's get into the first one, which is skill stacking. Skill stacking is basically this principle that these individual skills that may seem random to us at first, once you put them together, they form a stack. And in that stack is what gives us leverage into succeeding. Normally, when we talk about a jack of all trades, they're looked at as people who are aimless. They are not really focused in one specific area. And I think in today's society, we favor specialists. And in Natalie's journey, I think it's really evident that she was doing a lot of different things, right? She started off studying engineering. She became an influencer. And now she's doing a tech company, right? And all of these things in her journey, as we spoke about, seems so unbelievably random. But the funny thing is, when you really look at what she's doing today, all of them were serving her purpose. All right. Number two, speed to action. I think Natalie's biggest strength is that she doesn't overthink things. I know that in putting it in practice, it feels a little reckless. It feels like I'm not doing the research to actually do the right steps to get to where I want. But in reality, I think that honestly the thought before action sometimes is what screws the process up. I'll give you an example. Lately, we've been really trying to commit to one episode every single week. And at first, it really felt like what if the thought behind the videos, the teasers are going to suffer because we're doing this. But in reality, by not allowing myself to overthink it, what it really is doing is allowing us to test different things that will lead us to success. Which leads us to the success loop. This principle is basically that you take action, you fail, and then you pivot. And you do that over and over again until that failure turns into success. What we're seeing with the most successful people is that they go through the success loop over and over and over again. And they do it in short windows of time. We did an episode about Steve Jobs and he talks about how you want to shrink that time from idea to execution and the success loop is really the way to do that. All right, let's move on. The next principle is the skateboard principle. Basically, the skateboard principle is that if you have an idea, right, you don't want to rush to build the full thing. You want to start with something that's just going to get you to point B. So, let me explain. If you want to build a car, you shouldn't go about it by building a wheel and then another one and then a window. You should go about it by building a skateboard first and then a scooter and then a motorcycle and then a car. What you want to do is you want to build a product or an MVP that's going to allow you to get from point A to point B. In Natalie's case, I think what's really interesting in Reub 1.0, she didn't build the skateboard. The team was trying to build the car. You might remember that Natalie talks about how they were trying to build for everyone and they had all of these different features in their original app, but in reality, her original concept was about solving all of these problems, right? And what's interesting is it wasn't solving one problem. It was trying to solve too many. And that's really what the cart was. It's important that if you're just starting your passion project or your company that building a product that solves one specific problem is generally the way to start before you start adding all of these other features and services that ultimately will be your car. You want to narrow your tests. Really understanding what is really your final destination here, right? Great. Scale it all the way back and start with something smaller. I think that it's so easy nowadays to think that we have to do so many things to actually stand out. If you're building a product, you're not going to be able to successfully build the right features if you're doing and building all of them at the same time because by every feature and every complexity that we add to our initial project, we're creating just different success loops all at the same time and it makes it really hard to manage. The less complexity we add to our goals, the more likely that we're going to be able to go through that success loop effectively. The skateboard principle reminds us that we want to stay as simple as possible. But then's the question, which skateboard do I choose? What is the first feature or the first part of my project that I should really isolate my attention on? Well, I'll say that one way is to really understand what out of all of these things will give me the highest leverage to get to the next step. Meaning, if I'm trying to choose between which platforms I want to focus on, well, which one do I ultimately want to get to? And B, which is the one that if I were able to actually successfully get through the success loop would be more likely that I could get through the others. And if that doesn't make it clear for you, I do think that you have to understand that you're doing the best that you have with the information that you have now. Once you have new information that disproves that this is wrong, then you move on. But I do think that you have to give yourself time within that success loop to make sure that you've exhausted all options before you pivot. All right. Next, we have superstars. The thing about superstars is these are a players. These are individuals that you surround yourself with within your company or organization, within your career that will help you get to the next level. Superstars are proactive. They have initiative. They get [ __ ] done. And I don't just mean effective workers. I mean like these are actually people who are playing on the same playing field as you or ideally even better. Recently I watched a master class with Kim Kardashian where she was talking about something absolutely wild that she built KKW her one of her first makeup lines to a billion dollar company with four people. You would have thought that she hired all of these different people to actually build a company to get that valuation. And here's the thing in Natalie's story that I think is really important. Because she found the right superstars, she was able to keep her team small and nimble, which ultimately gave her more runway. It's also the reason that she was able to even build a company to the place that it's at right now. Steven Bartlett says that if you want to be a CEO or run something, you have to be vigilant about recruiting the right people who are going to increase your likelihood of success. And if you're in a position where you can't really like pay a lot of money and you think that you can't find a superstar, there are other things to offer. Remember, superstars are attracted to growth. And that growth can be financial growth. It can be career growth. It can be an opportunity to own something. You just have to think about the kind of a player you want and what their goal might be. All right. So, our next principle is strategic partners, which I'm going to go through very quickly. A strategic partner is different than a superstar in the fact that these individuals are people that you actually partner up with in your business, right? They either have capital to invest in, they have existing systems or audience or access to something that would be very difficult for you to acquire on your own. And in Natalie's case, that looked like an investor. And this investor not only gave her the capital that she needed but also helped her find other investors so that she could actually make this dream come true. And when we talk about the principle of strategic partnerships, what we realize is there is generally another principle that follows and that is undeniable proof. Undeniable proof is how you get the strategic partner. Now there are different ways to create undeniable proof but it's exactly what it sounds like. you are giving the proof so that people can't deny that this will be successful. And for Natalie, it was they created an MVP. They brought it to market and enough for them to get enough downloads where investors weren't doubting if it was actually going to work. They actually had the evidence to prove that it would. And I'll be honest, as our company, we have an app. And when we were going to investors, it was a lot harder to sell our app originally because we hadn't collected any users. We had no data. We had no undeniable proof and therefore no strategic partners were interested in investing in this. Strategic partners like certainty. So you need to provide that to them through this proof. All right, moving on to our next principle, which is your customer is your co-founder. Now, I'll be honest, this particular principle, I totally stole it from MasterClass uh with Kim Kardashian where she was talking about how her customers help her build her businesses, right? And she includes her customers in the choices that she makes for it. And what's interesting is that is exactly what we heard Natalie bring up over and over and over again in this interview. We actually saw in our last interview with Juan Meisel who quite literally didn't even build his MVP first. He just built it with his consumer. I think there's a lot of times that we have to be honest with ourselves that when we create things, how often are we actually thinking about ourselves and we get caught up in so much in what we want and what we believe people need and we forget to ask and look at what others really want. And I think that this applies to more than business. I think this applies to any dream that you are creating and understand how is it that me bringing this dream to life is going to affect other people. Natalie talks about building in public. Well, I think a lot of people build in public because they either think that they have to or because they just want to share their experiences. I think we get so wrapped up in finding the right superstars or doing the right things in order to build our dreams that we forget that the people that we're building our dreams for, they might have a lot more answers than anyone else. And by almost looking at your customers as your co-founder of your dream and actively reaching out to them and actively trying to get feedback, you are more likely to create something that's actually going to resonate. And look, if you are creating your dream to just express yourself or to just create something that you want to say, that's totally fine. But you have to understand that you are doing this for yourself. Now, if you're creating something for other people, then I think it's only right that we include them in our own dreams. At least that's how I feel about it. All right, moving on to our next principle, which is to evaluate the right metrics. You just have to understand how like connection and trust works. >> It's funny because you say the word trust a lot, but when we're dealing with brands, attention feels like the thing we're chasing sometimes. And so should their first thing be trust or is it attention? >> I'll start from the top. So I talk about the marketing funnel being dead. And the reason I talk about that and it's backed up in a lot of research and data and uh scientific papers is because it's not linear anymore. So what we used to have as awareness down into consideration down into conversion, it was a very sort of simple linear bucket that went straight through. Now in 2026, we have this concept of I guess discoverability is everywhere. So let's talk about this podcast for example. Someone may discover this podcast from a clip on Instagram. Someone may discover it when they're scrolling their YouTube algorithm looking for business advice. Someone may discover it when someone in their WhatsApp group chat sends it to them. So it's now not awareness, consideration, conversion. It's touch points of discoverability and it's how many touch points they need to experience of your brand before they convert. So it's messier. Then we think you have to think about the content funnel, right? So the content funnel still does exist and the content funnel looks like attention which leads to discoverability and understanding of the brand. Then you've got connection which is where someone like resonates, they see your content multiple times, they might convert into a follow. And then you've got sort of conversion into the brand at the bottom which is ultimately trust. So you've got like attention, connection, trust. That's sort of what the funnel looks like. So at the top you're not going after trust, right? Because trust takes time to build up. So the first time I see your content, I'm probably going to scroll past it in like two seconds unless it like hits with me. If it does, I'm probably not going to click on your page still because it's the first time I've seen it. If I get served it again and again and then my friend tells me about it and then I see it on another platform and then I see the founder talking about behind the scenes of the brand or an employee, I'm like, okay, there's like lots of moments here of capturing my attention. I'm going to go and have a look at the page. I'm going to go and maybe convert to the long form. And then over time the trust is built >> because trust is what happens when multiple touch points of your brand have reached your desired audience and you've offered them enough value to convert. So to answer your question, should brands be thinking about attention or trust because they feel conflicting? The top of the funnel that you need to think about is capturing attention always. And after capturing attention multiple times through various different touch points across multiple platforms, through your influence maps of discovery, you're going to build that trust. And I think that's why it's like so frustrating cuz, you know, I think the answer is always like, what's the key to social media? The key to it's always consistency, right? And how are we measuring success here? And it's really important that you get so clear on what you're measuring when you go in so that when it goes out and say it goes viral, you don't get distracted by that. Because you said earlier about um like the secret to social media success is consistency. Like of course it is like don't give up when everyone else would give up basically. But the actual formula for growth is consistency x experimentation because if you just keep being consistent, you will just plateau because you're not learning, you're not growing, you're not developing. You've got to find the opportunities for leveling up. So when the platform introduces a new feature, if you don't lean into it, you're going to get left behind. If a trend's happening that's super relevant to your industry, but you've got your content all lined up and batched for this month, then you're never going to experiment and find the opportunity to grow. It's like you've got to be looking at what's working, what's not, more importantly, and be adapting your content going forward. Because if you just keep posting, then eventually you're just going to flatline. You only get that hockey stick like compounding growth graph if you are willing to keep trying new things obsessively every single week on the level of consistency that you're like that's achievable for you. But how do you know which metric you should care about? Like we're facing this now. This is why I'm asking because we're so new. So I start with Grand Central like being the center of your city map and that has got to be your why. Your why is your anchor. It's where everything starts. It's where everything comes back to. It's how the whole flow works. Let's just do it for myself. My why is like be the intersection for creators and brands. So that's why people visit my city. That's why they show up. They want to learn about that topic. And I go, "Okay, where do I want people to go to when they're in my city?" So in New York City, you would go to Time Square, you would go to Statue of Liberty. And destination three, I want to grow my YouTube subscriber base to 100K. So if we want to get to the destinations, we need lines that take us from central station to the destination. So your lines are what I call your signals. What are the signals that's going to carry someone from your why through to the destination? So my signals for achieving this goal is that I need to increase my credibility. I need to demonstrate an ability to speak and I need to talk about unique topics. So when people think about booking a guest for an event, they think about me. For a book deal, I need to own certain topics. I need to really, really have authority on certain topics, they also need to see engagement and they need to see incremental growth because if they don't see engagement on my content, they don't see incremental growth. They don't think they're going to sell any books. And for 100K, I need to build awareness for my target audience. Currently, we've got all these people in Grand Central in your why. They've arrived because they're interested in your brand. But we need to know the right kind of people who we need to get onto each line. So now we need to think about the passengers for each line. So the passengers that we need to get on the speaking line are people who book speakers for events, corporates, like the type of people who I need to attract who are going to book me here. For my book deal, it's literary agents. It's like talent agencies. 100k is my target audience, which is people who want to build, ambitious people, people who are driven, and people who care about content. Immediately we've identified like the audience that we're going to take along each line. And then we need to think about the stops along the line that are going to keep people going. And the stops are your content formats. And we are trying to move people from the central area that they've arrived in and keep them on the line of interest. They take you to the destination and these are your content pieces that are going to do that. So it's like okay, what are the formats that are going to take a passenger along the line of credibility to reach the speaking goal? A lot of people think about the content they want to create and then they think, "Okay, how am I going to measure it?" They don't think about how it feeds into the wider network of your brand and how it's going to build towards a goal over time. So, it's all about goal, signal, content, it works backwards from there. So, when I think about that and I know that the signal is going to be credibility, I think, okay, the formats are going to be cut downs of me on podcasts. That's going to really help increase my credibility for speaking. It's going to be um short form videos where I can talk on a specific topic with a really good amount of credibility and it could even be written like it could be writing on LinkedIn but am I holding attention like am I able to hold attention on a certain topic repeatedly so those become my content formats and then what you need to do is you obviously need to create your timetable like how is this train system going to run the timetable becomes uh your content plan right so the timet says okay you're going to post two posts for the credibility line this week, two for the authority line and two for the attention line. And then you repeat it for a few weeks and at the end of the month you go, okay, have we taken people along this line? I.e., have we increased speaking? Have we had any inquiries about a book deal? Have we grown our audience closer to 100K? If yes, great. We keep doing more of the same thing. If not, we need to look at our timetable and shift it up. I redo this quarterly. And I think when you're not chasing like the accolades and you're chasing something that's a bit more meaningful and deeper and intrinsic, you can just keep going for a lot longer, you know, because otherwise what happens is you reach those like mountain tops and then you realize that it wasn't ever a mountain top and you just like the next mountains appeared in front of you and you just get too tired. You're like, I just can't keep chasing the next thing. But it was never about chasing. But I'm I'm actually very curious. I have to ask this. You talked about how it was hard to get guests at the beginning for anyone that is because I mean we're there, right? Steve never wanted to ask a guest to come on to the show, but I don't think without his existing audience, we would have ever been able to get any guests. I think it's almost impossible to start a podcast or a long form show without existing audience because getting people to trust you enough to spend 10, 20, 30 an hour with you without having that existing audience base, I think, is so hard. So whenever I see sort of people going, I just want to start a podcast, but they have like a thousand followers on Instagram. I'm like, that's going to be a hard climb. Why don't you work with someone who's got an existing audience because it's just going to make your life so much easier. But I mean, at the start, our whole mindset was it's a numbers game. If we message a,000 people, we're going to get 10 people who say yes. So we would message a,000 people every single week. And when we hit the limit on Steve's DMs, we would find their emails. Maybe they have a book coming out and it makes sense that they're on a press run and they will come and do your podcast cuz they're doing a load of other podcasts. And then eventually the person in the middle would be like, "Oh, I've seen that show. I've seen a lot of my friends go and I trust it. Oh, come on." It's the same marketing strategy that Triangle Bikinis used to get Kendall Jenner to wear their bikinis. I don't know if you've heard the story. >> No. >> So, this Australian bikini brand, they went completely viral uh in the late 2016. And they wanted Kendall Jenner to wear their bikini. Like, they knew that if Kendall Jenner wore it, they were going to go viral. They were going to take off. They needed her to wear it. They tried to get hold of Kendall. She obviously gets a million inquiries a day. So they were like, "How do we actually get to her?" So what they did is they found her circle of friends and they sent like 10 bikinis to all of her friends. All the friends start wearing the bikinis because they've been gifted it. They're not super famous. They're like, "This is so cool. These are nice bikinis." Kendall Jenner's team reached out to Triangle and are like, "Hey, um, Kendall really wants some bikinis. All her friends are wearing them. Like can you like sort her some out?" So they like infiltrated the friendship group. They built trust and they got proximity to her. What's an example of data that directly changed something big within the show? >> I think when YouTube introduced the retention graphs that was a big game changer for us. I think we play around a lot with understanding like where retention spikes would happen. It's all changed now, but at the time we had guests that would fit into four different categories. And we'd have understanding that within that category, retention spikes would happen at certain moments. So in the edit of the episode, for example, like on future episodes within that industry, we would lead with the retention spikes and try and make as many retention spikes that we could create within an episode. And that was a big game changer for us because we found that we were always starting episodes would tell us about your story. And actually, people just want to get to the action. They want to get to the advice. So we would uh Jack would create the edit so that that came first. I mean a huge thing was when we introduced like pre-production testing. So what we used to do at the very start was we put the episode live and we test two thumbnails and whichever one got more clickthrough rate was the thumbnail that it became. And we were like why do we wait until the out to test that? Because if we knew what people clicked on then we could make the title it. Then we could frontload the episode with that topic. Like we could do so much the episode if we knew that information before. So when we would record the episode, someone would be in the room, very manual at the start, writing down the lines that they thought were like the key lines based on understanding of what people click on. We put that all into a spreadsheet and turn them all into thumbnails. And all of those thumbnails would go into pre-production testing. So we'd understand what people were clicking on more than others. And sometimes you'd put like a title out and it would get like 20% more clickthrough rate. And our whole mindset was like, wouldn't it be such a shame if that podcast episode, which is the same podcast episode that they're going to get no matter what they click on, got 20% less clicks because we didn't know what the right title was. So, our whole mindset became kill the guesswork. We do meta ad testing. At some point, we were testing 100 thumbnails per episode. It was something that scaled. There's a whole team dedicated to it now. I mean, they've created softwares that automatically run the whole thing. So, it's it's a completely different space to what it was, but it was the same mindset. It was like, wouldn't it be a shame if this episode never saw the light of day because we didn't get the title right and we knew the episodes were valuable, but we wanted to work on increasing who saw it and the visibility towards it.
